Miracles

How Miracles Prove Prophethood and Their Certain Transmission
Al-Nasafi explains the proof-value of miracles and their transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle serves as proof of a prophet's truthfulness and why its transmission through tawatur yields certain knowledge.

Core Idea
God's enabling of miracles is His tacit confirmation of the prophet.

Al-Nasafi explains that a miracle is not just a wondrous event; it is God's affirmation of the one who performs it. Because the miracle defies natural laws and is witnessed by contemporaries who cannot replicate it, its report reaches us through tawatur, giving us certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the two distinct parts: the proof and the transmission.
2
Understand the proof: miracle = God's confirmation
Identify the phrase 'ysadiqqahu bima yuqaddiruhu 'ala fi'lihi mukhalifan li al-madda'. This is the core: God affirms the prophet by enabling something contrary to nature.
3
Recognize the role of witnesses and inability to oppose
Note that the miracles were witnessed by contemporaries who could not produce anything similar or oppose them. This strengthens the proof.
If they could oppose, the miracle would not be decisive.
4
Grasp tawatur and necessary knowledge
Learn that these reports were transmitted by tawatur (massive parallel transmission), which produces necessary knowledge—certain and requiring no further investigation.
This is why we can be certain of miracles even today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما وجه دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها فهو أنه تعالى يصدقه بما يقدره على فعله مخالفا للمادة. وبعد ذلك يتحدث النسفي عن بعض معجزات الرسل، وأن الله أيدهم بها، وشاهدها من عاشوا في زمانهم، ولم يستطيعوا الإتيان بمثلها، أو – معارضتها بأي وجه من الوجوه، ونقلت الأخبار بالتواتر الذي يوجب العلم الضروري.
As for the aspect of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it, it is that He Almighty affirms him by what He empowers him to do, contrary to matter. And after that, al-Nasafi discusses some of the miracles of the messengers, and that God supported them with them, and those who lived in their time witnessed them, and they were unable to produce anything similar, or oppose it in any way, and the reports were transmitted by tawatur which necessitates necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri).
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains how a miracle functions as proof and how we know it. The miracle indicates truthfulness because God, by enabling the prophet to perform something contrary to natural matter, essentially affirms his claim. Al-Nasafi then notes that these miracles were witnessed by contemporaries who could not replicate or oppose them, and the reports have come down through tawatur (massive parallel transmission) that yields necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri), which is certain and requires no further investigation.

Key Terms
دلالة
صدق
تواتر
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The miracle's proof is based solely on its wondrous nature, independent of God's role.
Correction: Al-Nasafi emphasizes that the miracle indicates truthfulness because God enables it, making it a divine confirmation, not just an inexplicable event.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how does a miracle indicate the truthfulness of the prophet?
A) By being impossible for humans
B) By God affirming him through enabling the miracle
C) By being reported by many people
D) By being useful

Key Takeaway
A miracle proves a prophet's truthfulness because God enables it, and its certain transmission through tawatur assures us of its occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in miracles because of tawatur. But if you truly had necessary knowledge of them, would your daily life reflect that certainty? What would change?
?
God affirms the prophet by enabling what is contrary to matter. When you read the Quranic accounts of miracles, do you consciously see God's hand affirming His messengers, or do you treat them as mere stories?
?
If a miracle were performed in front of you today, would you be more certain than you are of the miracles you've only heard about? What does that say about the strength of your faith in the transmitted reports?

Refuting the War Excuse: The Meccan Period
al-Nasafi refutes the claim that Arabs did not oppose the Qur'an due to being occupied with wars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the historical objection that Arabs were too busy with wars to oppose the Qur'an.

Core Idea
The Meccan period had no wars—so the excuse fails.

Al-Nasafi addresses a specific historical objection raised by Abu Isa al-Warraq, who claimed that the Arabs did not respond to the Qur'an's challenge because they were preoccupied with inter-tribal wars. This lesson examines al-Nasafi's concise refutation, which highlights the peaceful early period of the da'wah in Makka.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
First, grasp the claim of Abu Isa al-Warraq: the Arabs were too busy with wars to challenge the Qur'an. This is the context for the refutation.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key phrase 'فلم يكن بها حروب' (there were no wars there).
3
Analyze the refutation
Consider why al-Nasafi's point about the Meccan period is decisive. How does it show the objection is based on ignorance?
The refutation relies on historical fact: the early da'wah in Makka was peaceful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما ما يقوله أبو عيسى الوراق أنهم كانوا مشتغلين بحربهم، فهو كلام صدر عن الجهل ببدء الدعوة ، وكيف انتشرت ، وخاصة الفترة التي قضاها الرسول بمكة فلم يكن بها حروب، تحول بينهم وبين معارضته إذا كان في إمكانهم.
As for what Abu Isa al-Warraq says, that they were occupied with their wars, this is speech issuing from ignorance of the beginning of the da'wah and how it spread, especially the period that the Messenger spent in Makka; there were no wars there that would prevent them from opposing him if they were able.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
Al-Nasafi addresses a specific objection from Abu Isa al-Warraq, who claimed that the Arabs did not respond to the Qur'an's challenge because they were busy with wars. Al-Nasafi refutes this by pointing out that the early period of the da'wah in Makka was peaceful; there were no wars that would hinder literary opposition. Thus the excuse is invalid. The refutation is straightforward: the objection shows ignorance of the historical context.

Key Terms
أبو عيسى الوراق
دعوة
مكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the entire pre-Islamic period was war-filled, so the Arabs were too preoccupied to respond to the Qur'an.
Correction: Al-Nasafi points out that the Meccan period of the da'wah was peaceful, with no wars that would have prevented literary opposition. Thus the excuse is baseless.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi reject Abu Isa al-Warraq's objection?
A) Because the Arabs were not capable of opposition.
B) Because the Meccan period had no wars.
C) Because the Qur'an was not yet revealed in Makka.
D) Because the Prophet forbade warfare.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the war excuse by highlighting the peaceful Meccan period, showing that the objection stems from ignorance of history.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because you assumed others were too busy to oppose it? What if this same excuse hides your own reluctance to accept the Qur'an's challenge? Will you examine your heart honestly today?
?
When you encounter a refutation of a false claim, do you accept it with humility? Or do you cling to your assumptions out of ignorance? Al-Nasafi calls such ignorance 'كلام صدر عن الجهل'. How will you protect yourself from similar ignorance in your own beliefs?
?
The Meccan period was peaceful, yet the Quraysh rejected the Qur'an. Does your own lack of outward opposition to Islam indicate acceptance? Or is your heart hardened while you carry on with daily life? Will you confront your own resistance today?

Al-Nazzam's Theory of Sarfa and Poetry Analogy
Al-Nasafi presents al-Nazzam's theory then refutes it using an analogy with poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's claim that the Qur'an's inimitability is due to divine diversion (sarfa) and how al-Nasafi refutes it using an analogy with poetry.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's composition is inherently inimitable, not merely due to divine intervention.

Al-Nazzam proposed that the Qur'an is not miraculous in its wording but because Allah prevented the Arabs from matching it. Al-Nasafi begins his refutation by pointing out that even with access to the same words, no one can produce poetry of comparable quality, showing the composition itself is miraculous.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nazzam's theory
Read the first part of the passage where al-Nazzam's position is presented. Notice his claim that the Qur'an's miracle is due to sarfa (diverting), not its composition.
Al-Nazzam argues that individual verses are not miraculous, so the whole cannot be.
2
Understand the poetry analogy
Al-Nasafi compares the Qur'an to poetry: you have the same words as Imru' al-Qays but cannot produce a qasida like his. This shows the composition itself is the miracle.
The analogy targets the idea that diversion is unnecessary.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this analogy undermines sarfa. If the words are available but imitation fails, the inimitability lies in the arrangement, not divine prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرض لما يقوله النظام من أن القرآن ليس بمعجز النظم، لأن أفراد الآيات ليست بمعجزة، وما يصدق على الفرد يصدق على الكل وأن إعجاز القرآن راجع إلى الصرفة لأنه كان في إمكان القوم المعارضة إلا أن الله صرفهم عن ذلك، وهذا خرق للعادة. ويرد النسفي على النظام، موجها كلامه إليه بأن كل لفظة في شعر امرئ القيس في متناولك، ومع ذلك ائت بقصيدة مثل قفا نبك أو غيرها بل ائت بقصيدة من مثل قصائد من عاصرك، ولعل عجزه يؤكد بطلان كلامه عن القرآن. ويناقشه في الصرفة قائلا أفعل ذلك باختيار من العبد أم لا باختياره؟ فإن قال باختيار من العبد فهذا منه إثبات التصرف الله تعالى في أفعال العباد الاختيارية وهو محال عنده، وإن قال لا باختيار العبد فقد أثبت الجبر وهو لا يقول به. ويقال له أيضا : إذا صح كلامك لم لا يصرف همة الكافر عن الكفر حتى لا يقع فيه لما أنه أصلح له، لأنكم تقولون بوجوب الأصلح، ويستمر مع النظام بين السبر والتقسيم حتى يبطل كلامه بالصرفة.
And he presents what al-Nazzam says, that the Qur'an is not miraculous in its composition, because individual verses are not miraculous, and what applies to the part applies to the whole, and that the i'jaz of the Qur'an is due to sarfa (diverting), because the people were capable of opposing it but Allah diverted them from that, and this is a breaking of custom. And al-Nasafi responds to al-Nazzam, directing his speech to him: every word in the poetry of Imru' al-Qays is within your reach, and yet produce a qasida like 'Qifa Nabki' or others, nay produce a qasida like the qasidas of your contemporaries; perhaps your inability confirms the invalidity of your speech about the Qur'an. And he discusses with him regarding sarfa, saying: Did that happen with the choice of the slave or without his choice? If he says with the choice of the slave, then this is from him an affirmation of Allah's disposal of the voluntary acts of the slaves, which is impossible according to him. And if he says without the choice of the slave, then he has affirmed jabr (compulsion) which he does not hold. And it is also said to him: If your statement is correct, why does He not divert the intention of the disbeliever from disbelief so that he does not fall into it, since it is more beneficial for him, because you say that the best (al-aslah) is obligatory upon Allah? And he continues with al-Nazzam in examination and division (sabr wa taqsim) until he invalidates his statement about sarfa.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed refutation of al-Nazzam's theory. First, al-Nasafi reports al-Nazzam's position: i'jaz is not due to the Qur'an's composition (since individual verses are not miraculous) but due to sarfa—Allah diverted the Arabs from opposing it, which is a supernatural act. Then al-Nasafi refutes: he first argues by analogy using poetry. If you cannot produce poetry like that of Imru' al-Qays or even contemporaries, despite having the same words, that shows the composition itself is inimitable. Then he turns to the sarfa theory specifically, using a logical dilemma: the diverting (sarf) happened either with the free will of the Arabs or not. If with free will, then Allah is intervening in voluntary actions, which al-Nazzam would deny. If without free will, then it is jabr (compulsion), which al-Nazzam also rejects because he is a Mu'tazili who believes in free will. Thus the sarfa theory is self-contradictory. Additionally, if Allah can divert, why does He not divert disbelievers from disbelief for their own benefit, given the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah (the best)? Al-Nasafi says he continues with al-Nazzam using the method of sabr wa taqsim (examination and division) until he refutes the sarfa claim entirely.

Key Terms
الصرفة
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does al-Nazzam claim about the Qur'an's inimitability?
A) It is due to its composition.
B) It is due to divine diversion (sarfa).
C) It is not miraculous.
D) It is due to eloquence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's theory of sarfa is undermined by the fact that poetry cannot be imitated even with the same vocabulary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Qur'an recited, do you ever pause to consider that its very words and arrangement are beyond human capacity—and that this demands a response from you, not just intellectual acknowledgment? What would it take for you to truly submit to that reality today?
?
Al-Nasafi points out that you cannot even imitate a poet of your own time. Has the inability of all humanity to produce anything like the Qur'an ever struck you deeply enough to change how you approach reading it? If not, what is holding you back from that conviction?
?
If you believe that the Qur'an's inimitability is solely due to Allah preventing imitation, you might still feel you could 'match' it if given the chance. Do you harbor any hidden pride that you could produce something similar if Allah permitted? How does al-Nasafi's analogy dismantle that pride, and what will you do about it?

The Logical Dilemma of Sarfa and al-Aslah
Al-Nasafi uses logical division and the principle of the best to refute al-Nazzam's sarfa theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's logical argument against sarfa, which shows it leads to either compulsion or divine intervention in free will, and why the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah further refutes it.

Core Idea
Sarfa is self-contradictory because it implies either jabr or invalidates free will, both rejected by al-Nazzam.

Al-Nasafi continues the refutation by examining the nature of the divine diversion. He presents a dichotomy: either the diversion happened with the Arabs' free will or without it. Neither option is acceptable to al-Nazzam. He then adds the argument from al-aslah: if Allah can divert, why doesn't He divert disbelievers from disbelief for their own good? This challenges the Mu'tazili principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the dichotomy passage
Read the passage where al-Nasafi asks whether the sarfa occurred with or without the slave's choice.
This is a classic sabr wa taqsim method.
2
Understand both horns
If with choice, Allah intervenes in voluntary acts (unacceptable to Mu'tazila). If without choice, it is compulsion (jabr), also rejected.
Al-Nazzam, as a Mu'tazili, rejects both.
3
Consider the al-aslah argument
If Allah can divert, why not divert disbelievers from disbelief? This would be best for them, but it doesn't happen, contradicting the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah.
Al-aslah is a key Mu'tazili doctrine.
4
See the method of refutation
Al-Nasafi uses sabr wa taqsim (examination and division) to systematically refute the sarfa theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرض لما يقوله النظام من أن القرآن ليس بمعجز النظم، لأن أفراد الآيات ليست بمعجزة، وما يصدق على الفرد يصدق على الكل وأن إعجاز القرآن راجع إلى الصرفة لأنه كان في إمكان القوم المعارضة إلا أن الله صرفهم عن ذلك، وهذا خرق للعادة. ويرد النسفي على النظام، موجها كلامه إليه بأن كل لفظة في شعر امرئ القيس في متناولك، ومع ذلك ائت بقصيدة مثل قفا نبك أو غيرها بل ائت بقصيدة من مثل قصائد من عاصرك، ولعل عجزه يؤكد بطلان كلامه عن القرآن. ويناقشه في الصرفة قائلا أفعل ذلك باختيار من العبد أم لا باختياره؟ فإن قال باختيار من العبد فهذا منه إثبات التصرف الله تعالى في أفعال العباد الاختيارية وهو محال عنده، وإن قال لا باختيار العبد فقد أثبت الجبر وهو لا يقول به. ويقال له أيضا : إذا صح كلامك لم لا يصرف همة الكافر عن الكفر حتى لا يقع فيه لما أنه أصلح له، لأنكم تقولون بوجوب الأصلح، ويستمر مع النظام بين السبر والتقسيم حتى يبطل كلامه بالصرفة.
And he presents what al-Nazzam says, that the Qur'an is not miraculous in its composition, because individual verses are not miraculous, and what applies to the part applies to the whole, and that the i'jaz of the Qur'an is due to sarfa (diverting), because the people were capable of opposing it but Allah diverted them from that, and this is a breaking of custom. And al-Nasafi responds to al-Nazzam, directing his speech to him: every word in the poetry of Imru' al-Qays is within your reach, and yet produce a qasida like 'Qifa Nabki' or others, nay produce a qasida like the qasidas of your contemporaries; perhaps your inability confirms the invalidity of your speech about the Qur'an. And he discusses with him regarding sarfa, saying: Did that happen with the choice of the slave or without his choice? If he says with the choice of the slave, then this is from him an affirmation of Allah's disposal of the voluntary acts of the slaves, which is impossible according to him. And if he says without the choice of the slave, then he has affirmed jabr (compulsion) which he does not hold. And it is also said to him: If your statement is correct, why does He not divert the intention of the disbeliever from disbelief so that he does not fall into it, since it is more beneficial for him, because you say that the best (al-aslah) is obligatory upon Allah? And he continues with al-Nazzam in examination and division (sabr wa taqsim) until he invalidates his statement about sarfa.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed refutation of al-Nazzam's theory. First, al-Nasafi reports al-Nazzam's position: i'jaz is not due to the Qur'an's composition (since individual verses are not miraculous) but due to sarfa—Allah diverted the Arabs from opposing it, which is a supernatural act. Then al-Nasafi refutes: he first argues by analogy using poetry. If you cannot produce poetry like that of Imru' al-Qays or even contemporaries, despite having the same words, that shows the composition itself is inimitable. Then he turns to the sarfa theory specifically, using a logical dilemma: the diverting (sarf) happened either with the free will of the Arabs or not. If with free will, then Allah is intervening in voluntary actions, which al-Nazzam would deny. If without free will, then it is jabr (compulsion), which al-Nazzam also rejects because he is a Mu'tazili who believes in free will. Thus the sarfa theory is self-contradictory. Additionally, if Allah can divert, why does He not divert disbelievers from disbelief for their own benefit, given the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah (the best)? Al-Nasafi says he continues with al-Nazzam using the method of sabr wa taqsim (examination and division) until he refutes the sarfa claim entirely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think sarfa is a viable explanation without theological consequences.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows sarfa leads to either compulsion or denial of free will, both problematic for al-Nazzam.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what does the sarfa theory imply if the diversion occurred without the slave's choice?
A) Free will
B) Jabr (compulsion)
C) Al-aslah
D) Sabr

Key Takeaway
The sarfa theory collapses under logical scrutiny because it forces a choice between jabr and invalid free will, and contradicts the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral temptation, do you feel that Allah has compelled you to sin (jabr) or that you have free choice (ikhtiyar)? How does al-Nasafi's logical division challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions without resorting to fatalism? What change will you make today to own your choices before Allah?
?
Think of a blessing you desperately wanted but did not receive. How does the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah, and its refutation here, affect your trust that Allah always does what is truly best? Will you surrender your limited understanding of 'the best' to Allah's wisdom, even when it hurts?
?
Al-Nasafi subjects his opponent's belief to rigorous logical examination (sabr wa taqsim). What is one belief you hold about your faith that you have never deeply questioned? Are you willing to test it with the same honesty and courage? If you are afraid, what does that fear reveal about your attachment to that belief?

Refuting the Claim of Lost Imitations
al-Nasafi refutes the argument that imitations existed but were lost.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi rejects the argument that lost imitations of the Qur'an weaken its miraculous nature.

Core Idea
Lost imitations are no evidence—if they existed, they would have survived.

Al-Nasafi continues to defend the Qur'an's inimitability by addressing a final objection: some claim that opponents did produce imitations, but these were so suppressed by early Muslims that they were lost. This lesson unpacks his concise rebuttal and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the two key terms: معارضة (opposition/imitation) and اندراس (being lost/obliterated).
This is a single, dense refutation.
2
Understand al-Nasafi's argument
Identify the claim being refuted: that opponents did produce imitations but they were lost. Al-Nasafi invalidates this by implication: if such imitations existed, they would have been preserved by the Prophet's opponents as evidence against his prophethood.
The argument is logical, not empirical.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the complete absence of any competing text strengthens the proof of the Qur'an's inimitability. The onus is on the objector to produce at least one imitation.
This rebuttal completes the defense of the miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يبطل قول من يقول : إنهم عارضوه إلا أن المعارضة اندرست لكثرة اتباع الرسول.
And likewise he invalidates the statement of those who say: They did oppose it, except that the opposition was lost due to the large number of followers of the Messenger.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
Al-Nasafi briefly refutes another objection: that some Arabs did produce imitations of the Qur'an, but these were suppressed and lost because of the popularity of the Prophet. This is a weak argument because, as al-Nasafi implies, if such imitations had existed, they would have been preserved by opponents. The fact that no such imitation has come down to us shows that the claim is false. The refutation is concise.

Key Terms
معارضة
اندراس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that lost imitations undermine the Qur'an's miracle because opponents could not preserve them.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the absence of any surviving imitation actually confirms the miracle, as any such text would have been kept by opponents to discredit the Prophet.

Checkpoint
What is the claim al-Nasafi refutes in this passage?
A) That the Qur'an was not revealed to the Prophet.
B) That opponents did produce imitations but they were lost.
C) That the Prophet copied from earlier scriptures.
D) That the Qur'an is not Arabic.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's rebuttal shows that the complete absence of any surviving imitation proves the Qur'an's inimitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter objections to Islam that rely on 'lost' or 'hidden' evidence, have you ever accepted them without questioning the logic? How will you apply al-Nasafi's standard of preservation to test such claims in the future?
?
If the Quran is truly unmatched and no human words can equal it, what does that demand from you in your daily engagement with it? Are you treating it as an ordinary book, or as the decisive proof it claims to be? Commit to one concrete change in how you read or reflect on the Quran starting today.
?
Al-Nasafi's rebuttal exposes a common human tendency: to dismiss clear proof by appealing to missing counter-evidence. Where in your own life have you used similar excuses to avoid acknowledging a difficult truth? How will you stop seeking lost alternatives and face the truth directly?

The Second Aspect of Qur'anic Miracle: Prophecies and Eternal Laws
Al-Nasafi enumerates the second aspect of i'jaz: prophecies and eternal legal principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second aspect of the Qur'an's miracle: its reports of the unseen and its enduring legal principles that prove its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's prophecies and timeless laws show it comes from the Knower of the Unseen.

Al-Nasafi now presents another dimension of the Qur'an's inimitability. Beyond its linguistic beauty, the Qur'an contains accurate news of past nations and future events, as well as foundational legal rulings that remain valid until the end of time. This passage highlights how these features point unmistakably to a divine source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and the English translation once to get the overall flow. Note the two components mentioned.
2
Examine the two elements
Identify the two elements: news of past and future (إخبار عن الأمم الماضية وما يكون في المستقبل) and fundamental rulings until the Day of Resurrection (أصول الأحكام التي تقع إلى قيام الساعة).
These are the core of the second aspect.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that these clearly indicate the Qur'an is from the Knower of the Unseen (علام الغيوب). Reflect on why prophecies and eternal laws are strong evidence for divine origin.
No human could know the unseen or legislate timeless laws.

Source Text

٢ - ومن جوه الإعجاز القرآني ما فيه من الإخبار عن الأمم الماضية، وما يكون في المستقبل، وما أودع فيه من أصول الأحكام التي تقع إلى قيام الساعة، وهذا يدل دلالة واضحة على أنه من عند علام الغيوب.
2 - And among the aspects of the Qur'anic i'jaz is what it contains of news about past nations and what will happen in the future, and what is deposited in it of fundamental rulings that remain until the Day of Resurrection, and this clearly indicates that it is from the Knower of the Unseen.

Commentary
This passage lists the second aspect of the Qur'an's miraculousness. Al-Nasafi mentions two elements: first, its accurate reports about previous nations and future events (prophecies), which no human could have known. Second, it contains foundational legal principles that are valid until the end of time, showing its eternal relevance. Both indicate that its source must be Allah, the Knower of the Unseen (عالم الغيوب). This is a concise but powerful argument.

Misconception: Some think the Qur'an's miracle is only its linguistic eloquence.
Correction: This passage adds that its prophecies and comprehensive, eternal laws are also miraculous aspects proving divine origin.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's accurate prophecies and enduring legal rulings are clear proof that it comes from Allah, the All-Knowing.

?

?

?

Refutation of Abu Isa al-Warraq's Claim
Al-Nasafi refutes Abu Isa al-Warraq's excuse for Arab silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the objection that the Arabs were too occupied with wars to challenge the Quran.

Core Idea
The peaceful Meccan period disproves the war-excuse for failing to oppose the Quran.

Al-Nasafi addresses an objection that could undermine the miracle of the Quran. Abu Isa al-Warraq argued that the Arabs' lack of opposition was due to war preoccupation, not the Quran's inimitability. This passage dismantles that claim by pointing to the historical context of the Meccan period.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a refutation.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic.
2
Identify the objection
What claim does Abu Isa al-Warraq make? How would it weaken the argument for the Quran's miracle?
3
Analyze al-Nasafi's response
Al-Nasafi says the claim stems from ignorance. Why? Focus on the Meccan period mentioned.
The key point is that there were no wars in Mecca at that time.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Why does the absence of wars make the Arabs' failure to oppose the Quran significant?
This reinforces the Quran's inimitability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما ما يقوله أبو عيسى الوراق أنهم كانوا مشتغلين بحربهم، فهو كلام صدر عن الجهل ببدء الدعوة، وكيف انتشرت، وخاصة الفترة التي قضاها الرسول بمكة فلم يكن بها حروب، تحول بينهم وبين معارضته إذا كان في إمكانهم.
As for what Abu Isa al-Warraq says, that they were occupied with their wars, it is speech that emanates from ignorance of the beginning of the call and how it spread, especially the period the Messenger spent in Mecca, for there were no wars that would prevent them from opposing it if they were able.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection by Abu Isa al-Warraq. He claimed that the Arabs were too busy with their wars to produce a challenge to the Quran, thereby undermining the argument for i'jaz. Al-Nasafi counters that this claim stems from ignorance of the early history of the da'wa. He points out that during the Meccan period, the Prophet did not engage in war. Thus, the Arabs had ample opportunity to oppose the Quran if they could. The argument is that their failure to do so despite the absence of warfare proves the Quran's miraculous nature.

Key Terms

Misconception: The Arabs were incapable of opposing the Quran because they were constantly engaged in wars.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that during the Meccan period there were no wars, so this excuse is invalid; their failure to produce a challenge was due to the Quran's miraculous nature.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Arabs actually produced imitations.
B) Because the Meccan period was free of war.

D) Because the Arabs were not capable of poetry.

Key Takeaway
The Arabs' failure to oppose the Quran during the peaceful Meccan period demonstrates its miraculous nature.

?

An-Nazzam's Theory of Sarfa and Its Initial Refutation
Al-Nasafi presents an-Nazzam's theory and refutes it via the poetry analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an-Nazzam's theory that the Quran's miracle is due to divine prevention (sarfa) and al-Nasafi's initial refutation using the analogy of poetry imitation.

Core Idea
Imitation of individual words does not disprove the Quran's miracle.

An-Nazzam argued that the Quran's miraculousness is not inherent but due to God preventing the Arabs from imitating it. Al-Nasafi first points out a logical flaw: if one can imitate single words of a famous poem but cannot produce a whole poem like it, similarly the inability to imitate the Quran proves its miraculous nature. This sets the stage for a deeper critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow of the argument.
2
Identify an-Nazzam's premise
Notice that an-Nazzam claims the Quran is not miraculous in its composition because individual verses are not miracles.
He uses the logical fallacy of composition.
3
Analyze al-Nasafi's refutation
See how al-Nasafi uses the poetry analogy: if you can imitate a single word of Imru' al-Qais but cannot produce a whole poem, then the inability to imitate the Quran shows its miracle.
This undermines an-Nazzam's assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرض لما يقوله النظام من أن القرآن ليس بمعجز النظم، لأن أفراد الآيات ليست بمعجزة، وما يصدق على الفرد يصدق على الكل وأن إعجاز القرآن راجع إلى الصرفة لأنه كان في إمكان القوم المعارضة إلا أن الله صرفهم عن ذلك، وهذا خرق للعادة. ويرد النسفي على النظام، موجها كلامه إليه بأن كل لفظة في شعر امرئ القيس في متناولك، ومع ذلك ائت بقصيدة مثل قفا نبك أو غيرها بل ائت بقصيدة من مثل قصائد من عاصرك، ولعل عجزه يؤكد بطلان كلامه عن القرآن. ويناقشه في الصرفة قائلا أفعل ذلك باختيار من العبد أم لا باختياره؟ فإن قال باختيار من العبد فهذا منه إثبات التصرف الله تعالى في أفعال العباد الاختيارية وهو محال عنده، وإن قال لا باختيار العبد فقد أثبت الجبر وهو لا يقول به. ويقال له أيضا : إذا صح كلامك لم لا يصرف همة الكافر عن الكفر حتى لا يقع فيه لما أنه أصلح له، لأنكم تقولون بوجوب الأصلح، ويستمر مع النظام بين السبر والتقسيم حتى يبطل كلامه بالصرفة.
And he presents what an-Nazzam says, that the Quran is not miraculous in its composition, because individual verses are not a miracle, and what is true of the individual is true of the whole, and that the inimitability of the Quran is due to sarfa (prevention), because the people were able to oppose it except that God turned them away from that, and this is a breaking of custom. And al-Nasafi refutes an-Nazzam, directing his speech to him, that every word in the poetry of Imru' al-Qais is within your reach, yet produce a poem like 'Qifa nabki' or other, rather produce a poem similar to the poems of your contemporaries; perhaps your inability confirms the falsity of his speech about the Quran. And he debates him concerning sarfa, saying: Did that happen by the choice of the servant or not by his choice? If he says by the choice of the servant, then this is from him an affirmation of God's disposal in the voluntary acts of servants, and that is impossible in his view. And if he says not by the choice of the servant, then he has affirmed coercion (jabr), and he does not hold that. And it is also said to him: If your speech is correct, why does He not turn the disbeliever's resolve away from disbelief so that he does not fall into it, since it is more beneficial for him, because you say that the most beneficial is obligatory. And he continues with an-Nazzam between examination and division until he invalidates his speech about sarfa.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an-Nazzam's theory of i'jaz. An-Nazzam argued that the Quran's miraculousness is not inherent in its composition (nazm) because individual verses are not miracles and the whole is just an aggregate; rather, it is due to sarfa—God prevented the Arabs from producing a similar text despite their ability. Al-Nasafi first points out a logical flaw: if one can imitate individual words of Imru' al-Qais but cannot produce a poem like his, similarly the inability to imitate the Quran shows its miraculous nature, undermining an-Nazzam's premise. Then he poses a dilemma: Is the prevention (sarfa) with the servant's choice or without? If with choice, it implies God intervenes in voluntary human acts, which an-Nazzam (a Mu'tazili) rejects. If without choice, it implies coercion (jabr), which he also rejects. Further, if sarfa is true, why doesn't God prevent disbelief since it is more beneficial (as Mu'tazilites believe in obligation of the most beneficial). Thus, the theory is internally inconsistent.

Key Terms
النظام
الصرفة
الإعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran's miracle is only because God prevented people from imitating it, not because of its own excellence.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the inability to imitate despite imitable parts proves the Quran's inherent miraculous composition.

Checkpoint
According to an-Nazzam, why is the Quran not considered miraculous in its composition?
A) Because it is not possible to imitate it.
B) Because individual verses are not miracles and the whole is just the sum.
C) Because Arabs were not capable.
D) Because it was revealed in a miraculous way.

Key Takeaway
An-Nazzam's claim that the Quran's miracle is due to prevention is flawed; the inability to imitate a whole poem despite imitable parts proves the Quran's inherent miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or listen to the Quran, do you experience a deep sense of awe knowing that its composition is beyond human capacity, or have you grown numb to its miracle? What would it take for you to feel the weight of its inimitability in your heart today?
?
If the Quran's miracle is both in its text and in God preventing imitation, does that challenge you to engage more deeply with its words? Are you content to merely read it, or do you strive to embody a book that even the greatest poets could not rival?
?
The inability of the Arabs to produce a single sura is a historical fact. Does this fact move you to gratitude and humility, or do you take the Quran for granted? What concrete change can you make in your relationship with the Quran to honor its miraculous nature?

The Dilemma of Choice and Coercion in Sarfa
Al-Nasafi poses a dilemma about free will and uses the principle of obligation of the most beneficial to refute sarfa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's dilemma against an-Nazzam's theory of sarfa: either it involves the servant's choice, implying God's intervention in voluntary acts, or it does not, implying coercion (jabr), plus the argument from obligation of the most beneficial.

Core Idea
Sarfa leads to contradictions about free will and divine justice.

Al-Nasafi continues his refutation by posing a critical dilemma: Is the prevention (sarfa) with the servant's choice or without? If with choice, it implies God's disposal in voluntary acts, unacceptable to an-Nazzam as a Mu'tazili. If without choice, it implies coercion (jabr), also rejected. Additionally, if sarfa works, why doesn't God prevent disbelief since it is more beneficial? This shows inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the dilemma and additional argument.
2
Understand the dilemma
Note the two options: sarfa with choice or without choice. See the implications for each.
An-Nazzam cannot accept either.
3
Consider the argument from original
Think about why if sarfa is a general principle, God should prevent disbelievers from disbelief, but He doesn't.
This reveals a contradiction in Mu'tazilite theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he says by the choice of the servant, then this is from him an affirmation of God's disposal in the voluntary acts of servants, and that is impossible in his view. And if he says not by the choice of the servant, then he has affirmed coercion (jabr), and he does not hold that. And it is also said to him: If your speech is correct, why does He not turn the disbeliever's resolve away from disbelief so that he does not fall into it, since it is more beneficial for him, because you say that the most beneficial is obligatory. And he continues with an-Nazzam between examination and division until he invalidates his speech about sarfa.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an-Nazzam's theory of i'jaz. An-Nazzam argued that the Quran's miraculousness is not inherent in its composition (nazm) because individual verses are not miracles and the whole is just an aggregate; rather, it is due to sarfa—God prevented the Arabs from producing a similar text despite their ability. Refutation of the Lost Imitations Claim
Al-Nasafi refutes a specific objection against Quranic inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi rejects the claim that imitations of the Quran were lost due to the Prophet's followers, and how this strengthens the doctrine of i'jāz.

Core Idea
Lost imitations are a self-defeating excuse; opponents would have preserved them.

Some argued that the Arabs did produce imitations of the Quran but that these imitations were lost because the Prophet's followers were so numerous they suppressed or destroyed them. Al-Nasafi refutes this claim in a single concise sentence, arguing that the very abundance of followers cannot erase all evidence. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice how succinctly al-Nasafi dismisses the objection.
The phrase 'and likewise he invalidates' connects to the previous argument.
2
Understand the claim being refuted
Some claimed that imitations existed but were lost due to many followers. What does this imply about the Quran's inimitability?
This claim tries to explain away the absence of imitations.
3
Analyze the refutation
Why is this claim invalid? Consider that opponents of Islam would have preserved any imitations as proof against the Prophet. The loss of all such imitations is implausible.
Al-Nasafi relies on historical common sense.
4
Connect to i'jāz
This refutation supports the doctrine that the Quran is inimitable; no human could produce its like, and any alleged imitation would have been preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يبطل قول من يقول : إنهم عارضوه إلا أن المعارضة اندرست لكثرة اتباع الرسول.
And likewise he invalidates the statement of one who says: They opposed it, except that the opposition became extinct due to the abundance of followers of the Messenger.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This short passage refutes another objection to the Quran's inimitability. Some claimed that the Arabs did produce imitations, but those imitations were lost over time because the followers of the Prophet were so numerous that they suppressed or destroyed them. Al-Nasafi dismisses this as invalid. The implication is that if such imitations had existed, the opponents of Islam would have preserved them as evidence, and the many followers could not have erased them completely. The argument is concise but relies on historical plausibility.

Key Terms
اندرست
كثرة اتباع الرسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if imitations were lost, the Quran's inimitability is not proven because we don't have the imitations to compare.
Correction: Al-Nasafi points out that the very loss of imitations is evidence that they did not exist; opponents would have preserved them.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi rebut the claim that imitations of the Quran were lost due to many followers?
A) He says the followers would have preserved the imitations.
B) He implies the argument is self-defeating because the imitations, if real, would have been kept.
C) He argues that the Quran's miracle is not about imitation.
D) He says the followers destroyed them intentionally.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the lost imitations claim by asserting that if such imitations existed, they would have been preserved, not lost.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses to avoid a difficult truth, do you realize that your own preservation of evidence—or lack thereof—reveals your stance? What 'imitations' have you claimed existed but conveniently lost when challenged? Repent and align with truth.
?
If you had been among the opponents of the Prophet, would you have preserved an imitation if you had one? What does that say about your honesty? Examine your own use of excuses today: Are you hiding behind circumstances instead of admitting the truth? Commit to abandoning false justifications.
?
The opponents had every motive to preserve any imitation, yet none survived. What does this teach you about your own motives when you cling to weak excuses? Are you willing to let go of excuses and submit to the Quran's authority, even when it challenges you?

Prophetic News and Enduring Legal Principles
Al-Nasafi highlights the Quran's news of the unseen and its enduring legal principles as signs of divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's news of the unseen and its comprehensive legal principles demonstrate its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Quran's news and timeless laws prove it is from the Knower of the Unseen.

The Quran's inimitability extends beyond its literary excellence. Imam al-Nasafi points to two remarkable features: accurate reports about the past and future, and a set of legal principles that remain valid until the end of time. These aspects point directly to divine authorship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice that al-Nasafi presents two specific arguments: reports of the unseen and enduring legal principles.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find where al-Nasafi mentions news about past nations and future events, and where he mentions principles of rulings that last until the Day of Resurrection.
3
Reflect on why these indicate divinity
Ask yourself: Could a human prophet invent accurate news about the distant past and future? Could a human produce a legal system that remains valid for all time? The answer shows the Quran is from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - ومن وجوه الإعجاز القرآني ما فيه من الإخبار عن الأمم الماضية، وما يكون في المستقبل، وما أودع فيه من أصول الأحكام التي تقع إلى قيام الساعة، وهذا يدل دلالة واضحة على أنه من عند علام الغيوب. ومن دلائل الإعجاز أيضا: ما تضمنه القرآن من إشارات تدل على حدوث العالم، وبيان استحالة القول بإلهية من تعتريه الحوادث، وتتعاقب الأضداد عليه ودلالات ثبوت الصانع، ومحاجة اليهود والنصارى والمشركين، وبطلان القول بالطبائع من نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ قِطَعْ مُتَجَوِرَتٌ ﴾ [الرعد: 4] ودلالة إثبات البعث، وهو من الأمور الغيبية.
2. And among the aspects of the Quranic miracle is what it contains of news about past nations, and what will happen in the future, and what is deposited in it of the principles of rulings that will last until the Day of Resurrection, and this clearly indicates that it is from the Knower of the Unseen. And among the proofs of inimitability also: what the Quran includes of indications pointing to the temporal origination of the world, and the explanation of the impossibility of claiming divinity for one whom accidents befall and opposites alternate upon him, and proofs of the existence of the Creator, and argumentation against the Jews, Christians, and polytheists, and the invalidity of the theory of natures (taba'i') from the likes of His saying: {And in the earth are neighboring tracts} [Ar-Ra'd: 4], and an indication establishing resurrection, which is among the unseen matters.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage enumerates additional aspects of the Quran's inimitability beyond literary excellence. Al-Nasafi lists several: (1) Prophetic news about past nations and future events, which could not be known except by divine revelation. (2) The Quran's inclusion of fundamental legal principles (usul al-ahkam) that remain valid until the Day of Judgment, showing its comprehensive and enduring nature. (3) The Quran contains rational arguments: it points to the temporal origination (huduth) of the world, refutes the possibility of divinity for that which is subject to change and accidents, establishes proofs for the existence of the Creator (as-sani'), and debates against Jews, Christians, and polytheists. It also refutes the theory of natures (taba'i'), citing Quran 13:4 as an example. Finally, it provides evidence for resurrection, which is an unseen matter. These features collectively demonstrate that the Quran is from the Knower of the Unseen ('allam al-ghuyub).

Key Terms
الإخبار عن الأمم الماضية
أصول الأحكام
علام الغيوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran's news about past nations is just storytelling borrowed from earlier sources.
Correction: The Quran contains details not found in earlier scriptures and accurately predicts future events, proving it is from God.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what do the Quran's news of past nations and its legal principles together indicate?
A) The Prophet Muhammad was a historian.
B) The Quran is from the Knower of the Unseen.
C) The Quran is only a book of law.
D) The Quran imitates previous scriptures.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's accurate reports of the unseen and its timeless legal principles are clear proofs that it comes from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every news in the Quran about past nations and future events is absolutely accurate? How does that conviction affect your trust in its commands for your daily choices?
?
The Quran's legal principles are meant to last until the Day of Resurrection. Are you living according to them as if they are eternal, or do you sometimes treat them as outdated? What is one specific area where you need to realign your life with these principles?
?
If the Quran is from the Knower of the Unseen, then He knows your deepest secrets and your end. Does that thought fill you with awe and lead you to repentance, or do you ignore it? What change must you make today to take this truth seriously?

Rational Arguments in the Quran: From Creation to Resurrection
Al-Nasafi enumerates rational proofs within the Quran: temporal origination, refutation of false gods, evidence for the Creator, debates, refutation of naturalism, and proof of resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran contains rational proofs for the world's temporality, the existence of God, and the reality of resurrection.

Core Idea
The Quran provides intellectual arguments for God's existence, refutes false gods, and proves resurrection.

Beyond its linguistic miracle, the Quran presents compelling rational arguments. Al-Nasafi lists several: proofs for the temporal origination of the world, refutation of divinity for contingent beings, evidence for the Creator, debates against opponents, refutation of naturalism, and proof of resurrection. These show the Quran is not just beautiful speech but a source of intellectual guidance.

The Quran addresses theology, cosmology, and eschatology through reason. This diversity shows its comprehensive nature and divine origin.

Affirming Saints' Miracles: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Nasafi affirms saints' miracles and refutes Mu'tazila objections with examples and logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ahl al-Haqq affirm the miracles of saints (karamat al-awliya) and how they refute the Mu'tazila objections.

Core Idea
A saint's miracle confirms the prophet's truthfulness.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a theological dispute about the reality of saintly miracles. He presents the Mu'tazila argument against them and then defends the orthodox position, using historical examples and a logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Notice why the Mu'tazila deny saintly miracles: to avoid confusion with prophethood and because they see no benefit.
This is a key point of contention.
2
Examine the Ahl al-Haqq evidence
Nasafi cites two famous examples: the companion of Solomon bringing the throne of Bilqis, and Umar's clairvoyant command to Sariya. Consider how these are signs of sainthood.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi argues that the saint's miracle is actually a miracle for the prophet, as it confirms the saint's creed which is from the prophet. This resolves the Mu'tazila concern.
This is the core logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات كرامات الأولياء يبين النسفي أن المعتزلة يمنعونها حتى لا يلتبس الأمر بالنبوة، ولأنه لا فائدة لها. أما أهل الحق فيثبتونها ؛ لوجود أخبار كثيرة بذلك مثل صاحب سليمان، الذي أتى بعرش بلقيس قبل ارتداد الطرف، ومثل رؤية عمر لجيشه بنهاوند، وهو يقف على المنبر بالمدينة حتى قال يا سارية الجبل، وسمعه سارية. وبعد أن يذكر أمثلة كثيرة لذلك يناقش كلام المعتزلة، ويبين أن الهوى هو الذي ساقهم إلى ما قالوا، فلو أمعنوا النظر لعلموا أن كرامة الولي هي معجزة للرسول، إذ هي دليل على صدق عقيدة الولي، وعقيدته هي ما جاء بها الرسول ثم يفرق بين المعجزة والكرامة، ويبين أن الناقض للعادة ينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام أ - معجزة: وهي خاصة بالرسول. ب - كرامة وهي خاصة بالأولياء. معونة : وهي خاصة بالصالحين المؤمنين. د إهانة وتكون لمدَّعي الألوهية أو النبوة.
The discourse on establishing the miracles of saints (karāmāt al-awliyāʾ). Al-Nasafī explains that the Muʿtazila forbid them, lest the matter be confused with prophethood, and because they see no benefit in them. As for the people of truth (ahl al-ḥaqq), they affirm them due to the existence of many reports (akhbār) about that, such as the companion of Solomon who brought the throne of Bilqīs in the blink of an eye, and like the vision of ʿUmar of his army at Nihāwand while he was standing on the pulpit in Medina until he said: 'O Sāriya, [take] the mountain!' and Sāriya heard him. And after mentioning many examples of that, he discusses the speech of the Muʿtazila, and demonstrates that it is desire (al-hawā) that drove them to what they said. If they had investigated thoroughly, they would have known that the saintly miracle (karāma) of the saint is a miracle (muʿjiza) for the messenger, as it is evidence for the truthfulness of the saint's creed, and his creed is what the messenger brought. Then he distinguishes between a miracle (muʿjiza) and a saintly miracle (karāma), and explains that the breaker of custom (nāqiḍ al-ʿāda) is divided into four categories: a) a miracle (muʿjiza): it is specific to the messenger. b) a saintly miracle (karāma): it is specific to the saints (awliyāʾ). c) divine assistance (maʿūna): it is specific to the righteous believers (al-ṣāliḥīn al-muʾminīn). d) humiliation (ihāna): and it is for the claimants of divinity or prophethood.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage addresses the proof for miracles of saints (karāmāt). First, it presents the Muʿtazila position: they deny them to avoid confusion with prophethood and because they see no purpose. Then it presents the Ahl al-Haqq position: they affirm them based on numerous reports, citing examples: the instantaneous transportation of Bilqīs's throne by a companion of Solomon, and ʿUmar's clairvoyant command to his general Sāriya at Nihāwand. After examples, the author refutes the Muʿtazila, attributing their denial to desire (hawā). He argues that a saint's miracle is actually a miracle for the prophet because it confirms the truth of the saint's belief, which is exactly what the prophet taught. Finally, he classifies all supernatural events (nāqiḍ al-ʿāda) into four types: a) miracle (muʿjiza) for prophets, b) saintly miracle (karāma) for saints, c) divine assistance (maʿūna) for righteous believers, d) humiliation (ihāna) for false claimants of divinity or prophethood.

Key Terms
كرامات الأولياء
المعتزلة
أهل الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saintly miracles could be confused with prophethood and thus should be denied.
Correction: Saintly miracles are evidence for the prophet's truthfulness, so they do not cause confusion.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Ahl al-Haqq affirm saintly miracles?
A) Because they have no benefit
B) Because of many reports
C) To confuse prophethood
D) To contradict the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Saintly miracles are real and serve to confirm the prophet's mission, not to challenge it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a seemingly miraculous event in someone's life, do you instinctively attribute it to their own power or do you see it as a sign pointing back to the truth of the Prophet ﷺ? What concrete step can you take today to train your heart to redirect every wonder to its divine source?
?
The Mu'tazila denied karāmāt because they feared confusion. Is there any area of your faith where you hold back from accepting something simply because it might be confusing or uncomfortable? Could that hesitation be driven by your own desires (hawā) rather than pure fear of Allāh? What would it cost you to trust Allāh's reports fully?
?
Reflect on the story of ʿUmar's call to Sāriya. If you were in Sāriya's position and heard a voice from the unseen telling you to take the mountain, would you have obeyed instantly? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on Allāh's guidance through His righteous servants, and how can you strengthen that reliance starting today?

The Four Categories of Supernatural Events
Nasafi classifies breaker-of-custom events into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of 'breakers of custom' (nāqiḍ al-ʿāda) as classified by Nasafi: mu'jiza, karama, ma'una, and ihana.

Core Idea
Every supernatural event falls into one of four divinely ordained categories.

Having affirmed the reality of saintly miracles, Nasafi now provides a systematic classification of all events that break the natural order. This taxonomy clarifies the purpose behind each type and prevents confusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first category: mu'jiza
Define mu'jiza as the breaker of custom specific to prophets. It is a clear proof of prophethood.
2
Learn the second category: karama
Karama is specific to saints (awliya). It is a sign of their closeness to Allah.
3
Explore the third category: ma'una
Ma'una is for righteous believers. It is a form of Allah's help.
4
Examine the fourth category: ihana
Ihana occurs for claimants of divinity or prophethood. It is a form of disgrace.
This category shows that not all supernatural events are from Allah's honor; some are to debase false claimants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Classification of Miracles into Two Types
Nasafi classifies miracles into two types and explains the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of miracles according to Nasafi and how both involve a breach of custom.

Core Idea
Miracles are not only extraordinary acts but also preventing ordinary acts.

Nasafi begins his discussion of miracles by making a crucial distinction. Miracles are not limited to acts that break the natural order by doing something unusual; they also include preventing someone from performing a normal act. This classification broadens our understanding of what constitutes a miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two categories Nasafi mentions.
The passage is very concise.
2
Identify the two types
Find the two parts of miracles. What is the first? What is the second?
The first is 'unusual act', the second is 'incapacitation from usual act'.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Reflect on why even prevention is considered a breach of custom. How does Nasafi justify that both are essentially the same?
He says preventing the usual is also breaking custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعجز ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما: فعل غير معتاد. والثاني: تعجيز عن الفعل المعتاد، وهذا أيضا في الحقيقة نقض العادة إذ المنع عن المعتاد نقض للعادة وخروج عنها.
The miracle is divided into two parts: one: an unusual act. The second: incapacitation from the usual act, and this also is in reality a breaking of custom, for preventing the usual is a breaking of custom and a departure from it.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage classifies miracles into two types. The first type is an act that is not customary, such as bringing a dead person back to life. The second type is preventing someone from performing a customary act, which is itself a breaking of custom because it prevents what is normally possible. The author notes that both types are essentially breaches of the natural order.

Key Terms
معجز
فعل غير معتاد
تعجيز
نقض العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think a miracle must only be a positive act, but preventing an act is also a miracle.
Correction: Nasafi includes both unusual acts and prevention from usual acts as miracles, both breaking custom.

Checkpoint
How many types of miracles does Nasafi mention in this passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Nasafi teaches that miracles include both unusual acts and prevention from usual acts, and both are breaches of custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a sudden inability to do something you normally can, do you ever pause to consider that it might be Allah preventing you for a reason? How can you train yourself to see His hand in your limitations?
?
If every obstacle in your life could be a miracle of prevention from Allah, how would that change your reaction to failure or difficulty starting today?
?
You believe in miracles as unusual acts, but do you truly live as if Allah's prevention of ordinary acts is also a miracle? What would change if you took that seriously in your next disappointment?

Miracles

The Core Argument: Miracle as Divine Confirmation
Nasafi lays out the logical argument for how a miracle indicates truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reasoning by which a miracle serves as direct divine confirmation of a prophet's claim.

Core Idea
A miracle is God's direct 'yes' to a prophet's claim.

Nasafi begins by stating the fundamental question: how does a miracle indicate truth? He then lays out a logical argument based on God's knowledge and power. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step, showing that a miracle is equivalent to God saying 'you are truthful.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the logical flow: premise, claim, divine action, conclusion.
Notice the phrase 'نقض المادة' (breaking the natural order) which is key to understanding a miracle.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two premises: (1) God hears the claimant, (2) the miracle is beyond all human power. These are taken as known facts.
These premises are not argued; they are assumed as common knowledge.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God's performance of the miracle in response to the claim is a confirmation (تصديق) of the claim. Nasafi compares this to God directly saying 'صدقت' (you are truthful).
The comparison shows that the miracle is as clear as a verbal statement, but more powerful because it is an action.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is this argument important for establishing prophethood? How does it differ from other proofs?
This argument grounds the proof of prophethood in divine action, not human reasoning alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف تدل المعجزة على صدق الآتي بها؟ فأما وجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها، فإنا نعلم أن الله تعالى سامع لما يقوله هذا المدعي، وأن ما يظهر على يده خارج عن مقدور البشر بل عن مقدور جميع الخلق ولا قدرة عليه إلا الله تعالى، فإذا ادعى الرسالة، ثم قال آية صدق دعواي أن الله أرسلني أن يفعل الله كذا ففعل الله تعالى ذلك كان تصديقا من الله تعالى له فيما يدعي من الرسالة بما فعل من نقض المادة، فيكون ذلك كقوله عقيب دعواه هذه صدقت نظيره. وأن من أرسله غيره من البشر إلى آخر فقال الرسول للمرسل، اكتب لي شيئًا من الأسرار التي كانت بينك وبينه فكتب له، وعلم المرسل إليه أن لا اطلاع على تلك الأسرار لغير المرسل كان ذلك دلالة صادقة على صدق مدعي الرسالة. وكذا لو قال رجل لصاحب الوديعة بين يدي المودع، إن كنت صادقا فيما أدعى من أمرك إياي بأخذ وديعتك منك فناولني خاتمك فناوله كان ذلك دليلاً على صدقه، كما لو قال صدقت بل أبلغ إذ القول به يحتمل الاستهزاء وهذا مما لا احتمال فيه بوجه من الوجوه،
How does the miracle indicate the truthfulness of the one who brings it? As for the aspect of how it indicates his truthfulness, we know that God Almighty hears what this claimant says, and that what appears at his hand is beyond human capacity, indeed beyond the capacity of all creatures, and no one has power over it except God Almighty. So if he claims prophethood, then says, 'The sign of the truth of my claim that God sent me is that God does such-and-such,' and God Almighty does that, it is a confirmation from God Almighty to him of what he claims of prophethood, by means of what He did of breaking the material order. That is like His saying after his claim, 'You have spoken the truth' – its equivalent. And also, if a person sends another to someone else, and the messenger says to the sender, 'Write for me some of the secrets that were between you and him,' and he writes for him, and the one sent to knows that no one else has knowledge of those secrets, that would be a truthful indication of the truthfulness of the claimant of the message. Similarly, if a man says to the depositary in the presence of the depositor, 'If you are truthful in what I claim regarding your order to me to take your deposit from you, then hand me your ring,' and he hands it to him, that would be evidence of his truthfulness, just as if he said, 'You have spoken the truth,' but rather more, because speech can be interpreted as mockery, whereas this has no possibility of that in any way.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage explains how a miracle proves the truthfulness of a prophet. The author begins by stating the question: how does a miracle indicate truth? He then provides a logical argument: we know that God hears the claimant, and the miracle is beyond all human power, so when a prophet claims prophethood and performs a miracle, that miracle is a confirmation from God. He compares it to God saying 'you are truthful' directly. Then he gives two analogies to illustrate: first, a human messenger who proves his message by revealing secrets known only to the sender and receiver. Second, a man who proves his claim to have been entrusted with a deposit by asking the depositor to hand over a ring in front of the depositary, which serves as clear evidence because speech can be ambiguous but action cannot. The analogies show that a miracle is a decisive sign of truth.

Key Terms
معجزة
صدق
تصديق
نقض المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a miracle is just an impressive event that can be attributed to chance or magic.
Correction: Nasafi argues that a miracle is a deliberate divine act in response to a claim, making it a direct testimony from God, unlike magic which is within human capability.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the primary reason a miracle indicates the truth of a prophet?
A) The miracle is amazing and attracts followers.
B) The miracle is beyond human capacity and God performs it in response to the claim.
C) The prophet is known to be pious.
D) The miracle occurs in a public place.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is God's decisive confirmation of a prophet's claim, as clear as if God spoke directly.

Two Analogies: Why Actions Overcome Doubt
Nasafi gives two analogies to show why a miracle is more convincing than speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogies of the secret messenger and the depositor illustrate why a miracle is more convincing than mere speech.

Core Idea
Actions speak louder than words, and miracles are God's actions.

To make the logic more concrete, Nasafi provides two analogies from human interactions. These analogies highlight how a miracle, being an action beyond human power, leaves no room for doubt. In both cases, an action (revealing secrets or handing over a ring) serves as undeniable proof, unlike speech which can be ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Read the two analogies
Read the Arabic and translation for both analogies. Try to visualize each scenario.
The first analogy involves a messenger and secrets; the second involves a deposit and a ring.
2
Understand the first analogy
A messenger proves his mission by revealing secrets only the sender and receiver know. Similarly, a prophet performs a miracle that only God can do.
The key point is that the secret is exclusive knowledge; the miracle is exclusive power.
3
Understand the second analogy
A man proves his claim by asking the depositor to hand over a ring in front of the depositary. The action is decisive proof.
Nasafi notes that speech can be interpreted as mockery, but an action cannot.
4
Compare the analogies to a miracle
Notice how both analogies involve an action that is beyond the ability of an impostor. The miracle is God's action, leaving no doubt.
Think about why Nasafi chose these specific analogies. They highlight certainty.

Two Views on Why Miracles Cannot Appear on False Prophets
The author presents two views on the impossibility of miracles appearing on false claimants to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main theological explanations for why miracles cannot be performed by false prophets, and why the verifiers prefer the Ash'ari explanation.

Core Idea
Miracles are exclusive to true prophets because they are inherently tied to truthfulness.

In this passage, the author examines a key question in prophethood: what prevents a miracle from appearing at the hands of a false claimant? He reports two competing answers from the Ash'ari school and some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith. The first emphasizes the implications for God's power, while the second rests on the intrinsic nature of miracles. Understanding these arguments clarifies the relationship between miracles and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very dense style with technical terms.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two distinct views. Find where each begins and note the reasoning behind each.
The first view is attributed to the majority of Ash'aris and some mutakallimun; the second to some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith.
3
Analyze the first view
The first view argues that a miracle on a liar would imply God's incapacity (ta'jiz) to provide proofs for the truthful prophet. Since God's incapacity is impossible, the miracle is impossible.
Note the qualification: if the claimant's person already indicates his lie, the miracle does not incapacitate God.
4
Analyze the second view
The second view argues that the miracle is intrinsically a proof of truthfulness, so it cannot coexist with falsehood, like a precise act being a proof of knowledge.
This view is more direct and does not involve God's attributes directly.
5
Consider the author's conclusion
The author states that the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among them adopt the first view. Reflect on why this might be the stronger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب جمهور الأشعرية ومن قال من متكلمي أهل الحديث : إنه لا سفه إلا ما ورد عنه النهي، إلى أن جهة امتناع ظهور الناقض للعادة على يدي مدعي الرسالة كاذبًا، أن ظهوره على يديه يوجب تعجيز الباري جلا وعلا عن إقامة الدلائل على صدق الصادق في دعوى الرسالة، وإثبات التفرقة بين الحق والباطل، وتعجيز الباري جل وعلا محال، حتى إن إظهار ذلك على يدي المتأله لما لم يكن موجبًا تعجيزه جل وعلا عن ذلك لما في شخص المدعي ما يدل على كذبه لم يكن ذلك ممتنعا. وقال بعض متكلمي أهل الحديث : أن جهة امتناع ذلك أن المعجزة دلالة الصدق لعينها، فلا يجوز وجودها مع غير الصدق كالفعل المحكم دلالة العلم لعينه فلم يجز اقترانه بالجهل فكذا هذا. والمحققون منهم على الوجه الأول.
The majority of the Ash'aris and those who said from among the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith, 'There is no foolishness except what is forbidden by revelation,' held that the aspect of preventing the appearance of a miracle (naaqid lil-‘aadah) at the hands of a claimant to prophethood who is lying is that its appearance at his hands necessitates rendering the Exalted and Majestic Creator incapable (ta‘jiz) of establishing proofs of the truthfulness of the truthful one in the claim of prophethood, and of establishing the distinction between truth and falsehood, and incapacitating the Exalted and Majestic Creator is impossible (muhaal). Thus, if the manifestation of that at the hands of one who claims divinity did not necessitate incapacitating Him—because in the person of the claimant there is something indicating his lie—then it would not be prevented. And some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith said: The aspect of preventing that is that the miracle (mu‘jizah) is a proof (dalaalah) of truthfulness by its very essence, so it is not permissible for it to exist with untruth, just as the precise act (fi‘l muhkam) is a proof of knowledge by its essence, so its association with ignorance is not permissible—so likewise this. And the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among them are upon the first view.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage presents two competing explanations for why a miracle cannot occur at the hands of a false claimant to prophethood. The author first attributes the majority view to the Ash'ari school and some mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith. Their reasoning is that if a miracle appeared on a liar, it would imply that God is incapable of providing distinctive proofs to confirm the truthful prophet and distinguish truth from falsehood. Since God's incapacity is impossible, the appearance of a miracle on a liar is impossible. However, the author qualifies this by noting that if the claimant's person already contains evidence of his lie (like claiming divinity without basis), then the miracle's appearance would not incapacitate God, so it would not be impossible. The second view, attributed to some other mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith, is more direct: the miracle itself is intrinsically a proof of truthfulness, so it cannot coexist with falsehood. They draw an analogy with a precise act (fi‘l muhkam), which is inherently a proof of knowledge and cannot accompany ignorance. The author concludes that the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among them adopt the first view.

Key Terms
المعجزة
التعجيز
دلالة الصدق
الناقض للعادة
الفعل المحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of a miracle on a liar is due to some physical impossibility, but it is actually a theological necessity based on God's attribute of power.
Correction: The impossibility is not physical but theological: either it would imply God's incapacity (first view) or miracles are intrinsically tied to truthfulness (second view).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented, why is it impossible for a miracle to appear on a false prophet?

B) Because it would imply that God is incapable of distinguishing truth from falsehood.
C) Because the false prophet lacks the moral purity required for miracles.
D) Because the crowd would be deceived and this would lead to chaos.

Key Takeaway
This passage demonstrates that the impossibility of miracles on false prophets is grounded either in God's attribute of power (preferred view) or in the intrinsic nature of miracles as proofs of truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The second view compares a miracle to a precise act that inherently proves knowledge. Think of a time you recognized someone's skill or honesty through their actions. How does that deepen your appreciation for the miracles of the prophets? Are you as quick to recognize divine signs as you are human ones?

The majority view says that a miracle on a liar would imply God's incapacity—a theological impossibility. What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that?
